         9,852,924 km (6,122,323 mi)
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
          Saturn
         </a>
         orbiter
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Orbital insertion
        </th>
        <td>
         July 1, 2004, 02:48 UTC
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
          Titan
         </a>
         lander
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Spacecraft component
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)">
           Huygens
          </a>
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         Landing date
        </th>
        <td>
         January 14, 2005
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr style="display:none">
        <td colspan="2">
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <div class="" style="width:100%; text-align:center; border-top:1px #aaa solid">
          <a href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">
           Flagship Program
          </a>
         </div>
         <div style="width:100%">
          <div class="noprint" style="float: left; text-align:left;padding-right:0.5em;">
           ←
           <i>
            <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
             Galileo
            </a>
           </i>
          </div>
          <div class="noprint" style="float: right; text-align:right;padding-left:0.5em;">
           <a href="/wiki/Mars_Science_Laboratory" title="Mars Science Laboratory">
            Mars Science Laboratory
           </a>
           →
          </div>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        <b>
         Cassini–Huygens
        </b>
       </i>
       mission (
       <span class="nowrap">
        <span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt">
         <a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">
          /
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="'k' in 'kind'">
            k
           </span>
           <span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">
            ə
           </span>
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'s' in 'sigh'">
            s
           </span>
           <span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">
            iː
           </span>
           <span title="'n' in 'nigh'">
            n
           </span>
           <span title="/i/: 'y' in 'happy'">
            i
           </span>
          </span>
          <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted">
           <span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">
            ˈ
           </span>
           <span title="'h' in 'hi'">
            h
           </span>
           <span title="/ɔɪ/: 'oi' in 'choice'">
            ɔɪ
           </span>
           <span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">
            ɡ
           </span>
           <span title="/ən/: 'on' in 'button'">
            ən
           </span>
           <span title="'z' in 'zoom'">
            z
           </span>
          </span>
          /
         </a>
        </span>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key">
        <i title="English pronunciation respelling">
         kə-
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          SEE
         </span>
         -nee
         <span style="font-size:90%">
          HOY
         </span>
         -gənz
        </i>
       </a>
       ), commonly called
       <i>
        <b>
         Cassini
        </b>
       </i>
       , was a collaboration between
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       (ESA), and the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Space_Agency" title="Italian Space Agency">
        Italian Space Agency
       </a>
       (ASI) to send a probe to study the planet
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       and its system, including its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_rings" title="Saturn's rings">
        rings
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">
        natural satellites
       </a>
       . The
       <a href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">
        Flagship
       </a>
       -class
       <a href="/wiki/Robotic_spacecraft" title="Robotic spacecraft">
        robotic
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">
        spacecraft
       </a>
       comprised both NASA's
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       probe, and ESA's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)">
         Huygens
        </a>
       </i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lander_(spacecraft)" title="Lander (spacecraft)">
        lander
       </a>
       which landed on
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn's
       </a>
       largest moon,
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flagship_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-flagship-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was the fourth
       <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">
        space probe
       </a>
       to visit
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       and the first to enter its orbit. The craft were named after astronomers
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">
        Giovanni Cassini
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">
        Christiaan Huygens
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Launched aboard a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Titan_IVB" title="Titan IVB">
        Titan IVB/Centaur
       </a>
       on October 15, 1997,
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was active in space for nearly 20 years, with 13 years spent orbiting Saturn, studying the planet and its system after
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orbital_insertion" title="Orbital insertion">
        entering orbit
       </a>
       on July 1, 2004.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20151218-jc_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20151218-jc-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The voyage to
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       included
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">
        flybys
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
        Venus
       </a>
       (April 1998 and July 1999),
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       (August 1999), the
       <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">
        asteroid
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/2685_Masursky" title="2685 Masursky">
        2685 Masursky
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       (December 2000). Its mission ended on September 15, 2017, when
       <i>
        Cassini's
       </i>
       trajectory took it into Saturn's upper atmosphere and it burned up
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170908_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170908-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       in order to prevent any risk of contaminating Saturn's moons, which might have offered habitable environments to stowaway terrestrial microbes on the spacecraft.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bizinside20170405_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bizinside20170405-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-divein_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-divein-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The mission is widely perceived to have been successful beyond expectation.
       <i>
        Cassini-Huygens
       </i>
       has been described by
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA's
       </a>
       Planetary Science Division Director as a "mission of firsts",
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       that has revolutionized human understanding of the
       <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">
        Saturn
       </a>
       system, including its moons and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saturn%27s_rings" title="Saturn's rings">
        rings
       </a>
       , and our understanding of where life might be found in the
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s original mission was planned to last for four years, from June 2004 to May 2008. The mission was extended for another two years until September 2010, branded the
       <i>
        Cassini Equinox Mission
       </i>
       . The mission was extended a second and final time with the
       <i>
        Cassini Solstice Mission
       </i>
       , lasting another seven years until September 15, 2017, on which date
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was de-orbited to burn up in Saturn's upper atmosphere.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Huygens
       </i>
       module traveled with
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       until its separation from the probe on December 25, 2004; it was landed by
       <a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">
        parachute
       </a>
       on
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">
        Titan
       </a>
       on January 14, 2005. It returned data to Earth for around 90 minutes, using the orbiter as a relay. This was the first
       <a href="/wiki/Landing" title="Landing">
        landing
       </a>
       ever accomplished in the outer
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">
        Solar System
       </a>
       and the first landing on a moon other than our own.
      </p>
      <p>
       At the end of its mission, the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       spacecraft executed the "Grand Finale" of its mission: a number of risky passes through the gaps between Saturn and Saturn's inner rings.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA-20170915_4-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA-20170915-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20170914_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-NYT-20170914-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The purpose of this phase was to maximize
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        's
       </span>
       scientific outcome before the spacecraft was disposed.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Cassini_retirement" title="Cassini retirement">
        atmospheric entry
       </a>
       of
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       ended the mission, but analyses of the returned data will continue for many years.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
        Overview
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Teams from 28 countries made up the joint team responsible for designing, building, flying and collecting data from the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       orbiter and
       <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)">
        <i>
         Huygens
        </i>
        probe
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The mission was managed by
       <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">
        NASA
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory" title="Jet Propulsion Laboratory">
        Jet Propulsion Laboratory
       </a>
       in the United States, where the orbiter was assembled.
       <i>
        Huygens
       </i>
       was developed by the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Research_and_Technology_Centre" title="European Space Research and Technology Centre">
        European Space Research and Technology Centre
       </a>
       . The Centre's prime contractor,
       <a href="/wiki/A%C3%A9rospatiale" title="Aérospatiale">
        Aérospatiale
       </a>
       of France (now
       <a href="/wiki/Thales_Alenia_Space" title="Thales Alenia Space">
        Thales Alenia Space
       </a>
       ), assembled the probe with equipment and instruments supplied by many European countries (
       <i>
        Huygens
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       batteries and two scientific instruments by the United States). The
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Space_Agency" title="Italian Space Agency">
        Italian Space Agency
       </a>
       (ASI) provided the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       orbiter's
       <a href="/wiki/Directional_antenna" title="Directional antenna">
        high-gain radio antenna
       </a>
       , with the incorporation of a low-gain antenna (to ensure telecommunications with the Earth for the entire duration of the mission), a compact and lightweight
       <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">
        radar
       </a>
       , which also uses the high-gain antenna and serves as a
       <a href="/wiki/Synthetic-aperture_radar" title="Synthetic-aperture radar">
        synthetic-aperture radar
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Radar_altimeter" title="Radar altimeter">
        radar altimeter
       </a>
       , a
       <a href="/wiki/Radiometer" title="Radiometer">
        radiometer
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Radio_science_subsystem" title="Radio science subsystem">
        radio science subsystem
       </a>
       (RSS), the
       <a href="/wiki/Visible_spectrum" title="Visible spectrum">
        visible
       </a>
       channel portion VIMS-V of VIMS
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">
        spectrometer
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASI-Cassini_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ASI-Cassini-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The VIMS infrared counterpart was provided by NASA, as well as
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Main_Electronic_Assembly&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Main Electronic Assembly (page does not exist)">
        Main Electronic Assembly
       </a>
       , which includes electronic subassemblies provided by
       <a href="/wiki/CNES" title="CNES">
        CNES
       </a>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">
        France
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MillerEA1996_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-MillerEA1996-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReiningerFM1994_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ReiningerFM1994-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On April 16, 2008, NASA announced a two-year extension of the funding for ground operations of this mission, at which point it was renamed the Cassini Equinox Mission.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExtensionTo2010_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ExtensionTo2010-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The round of funding was again extended in February 2010 with the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cassini_Solstice_Mission" title="Cassini Solstice Mission">
        Cassini Solstice Mission
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">
        Naming
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Naming">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="954" data-file-width="714" height="294" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg/220px-Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg/330px-Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg/440px-Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Huygens_Systema_Saturnium.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Huygens' explanation for the aspects of Saturn,
         <i>
          Systema Saturnium
         </i>
         (1659)
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The mission consisted of two main elements: the ASI/NASA
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       orbiter, named for the
       <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">
        Italian
       </a>
       astronomer
       <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Domenico_Cassini" title="Giovanni Domenico Cassini">
        Giovanni Domenico Cassini
       </a>
       , discoverer of
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
        Saturn's ring
       </a>
       divisions and four of its satellites; and the ESA-developed
       <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)">
        <i>
         Huygens
        </i>
        probe
       </a>
       , named for the Dutch astronomer, mathematician and physicist
       <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">
        Christiaan Huygens
       </a>
       , discoverer of Titan.
      </p>
      <p>
       The mission was commonly called Saturn Orbiter Titan Probe (SOTP) during gestation, both as a
       <a href="/wiki/Mariner_Mark_II" title="Mariner Mark II">
        Mariner Mark II
       </a>
       mission and generically.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini-Huygens
       </i>
       was a
       <a href="/wiki/Flagship_Program" title="Flagship Program">
        Flagship-class mission
       </a>
       to the outer planets.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flagship_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-flagship-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The other planetary flagships include
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
         Galileo
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">
        Voyager
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Viking_program" title="Viking program">
        Viking
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-flagship_7-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-flagship-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Objectives">
        Objectives
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Objectives">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       had several objectives, including:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Determining the three-dimensional structure and dynamic behavior of the
        <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">
         rings of Saturn
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Determining the composition of the
        <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">
         satellite
        </a>
        surfaces and the geological history of each object.
       </li>
       <li>
        Determining the nature and origin of the dark material on
        <a href="/wiki/Iapetus_(moon)" title="Iapetus (moon)">
         Iapetus
        </a>
        's leading hemisphere.
       </li>
       <li>
        Measuring the three-dimensional structure and dynamic behavior of the
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
         magnetosphere
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Studying the dynamic behavior of Saturn's
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_body_atmosphere" title="Celestial body atmosphere">
         atmosphere
        </a>
        at cloud level.
       </li>
       <li>
        Studying the time variability of Titan's clouds and
        <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">
         hazes
        </a>
        .
       </li>
       <li>
        Characterizing Titan's surface on a regional scale.
       </li>
      </ul>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini–Huygens
       </i>
       was launched on October 15, 1997, from
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station">
        Cape Canaveral Air Force Station
       </a>
       's
       <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral_Air_Force_Station_Space_Launch_Complex_40" title="Cape Canaveral Air Force Station Space Launch Complex 40">
        Space Launch Complex 40
       </a>
       using a
       <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">
        U.S. Air Force
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_IV" title="Titan IV">
        Titan IV
       </a>
       B/
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_(rocket)" title="Centaur (rocket)">
        Centaur
       </a>
       rocket. The complete launcher was made up of a two-stage
       <a href="/wiki/Titan_IV" title="Titan IV">
        Titan IV
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Booster_rocket" title="Booster rocket">
        booster rocket
       </a>
       , two strap-on solid
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rocket_motor" title="Rocket motor">
        rocket motors
       </a>
       , the Centaur upper stage, and a payload enclosure, or fairing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The total cost of this scientific exploration mission was about US$3.26
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1000000000_(number)" title="1000000000 (number)">
        billion
       </a>
       , including $1.4 billion for pre-launch development, $704
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Million" title="Million">
        million
       </a>
       for mission operations, $54 million for tracking and $422 million for the launch vehicle. The United States contributed $2.6 billion (80%), the ESA $500 million (15%), and the ASI $160 million (5%).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, these figures are from the press kit which was prepared in October 2000. They do not include inflation over the course of a very long mission, nor do they include the cost of the extended missions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The primary mission for
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was completed on July 30, 2008. The mission was extended to June 2010 (
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       Equinox Mission).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
        <a href="#cite_note-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This studied the Saturn system in detail during the planet's equinox, which happened in August 2009.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ExtensionTo2010_18-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ExtensionTo2010-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       On February 3, 2010, NASA announced another extension for
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       , lasting 6​
       <span class="frac nowrap">
        <sup>
         1
        </sup>
        ⁄
        <sub>
         2
        </sub>
       </span>
       years until 2017, ending at the time of summer solstice in Saturn's northern hemisphere (
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       Solstice Mission). The extension enabled another 155 revolutions around the planet, 54 flybys of Titan and 11 flybys of
       <a href="/wiki/Enceladus" title="Enceladus">
        Enceladus
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In 2017, an encounter with Titan changed its orbit in such a way that, at closest approach to Saturn, it was only 3,000 km above the planet's cloudtops, below the inner edge of the
       <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn#D_Ring" title="Rings of Saturn">
        D ring
       </a>
       . This sequence of "proximal orbits" ended when its final encounter with Titan sent the probe into Saturn's atmosphere to be destroyed.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Itinerary">
        Itinerary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Itinerary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>

             <img alt="Phoebe cassini.jpg" data-file-height="1184" data-file-width="813" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Phoebe_cassini.jpg/69px-Phoebe_cassini.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Phoebe_cassini.jpg/103px-Phoebe_cassini.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Phoebe_cassini.jpg/137px-Phoebe_cassini.jpg 2x" width="69"/>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
         </td>
         <td>
          <div class="center">
           <div class="floatnone">
            <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA12714_Janus_crop.jpg">
             <img alt="PIA12714 Janus crop.jpg" data-file-height="822" data-file-width="988" height="83" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/PIA12714_Janus_crop.jpg/100px-PIA12714_Janus_crop.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/PIA12714_Janus_crop.jpg/150px-PIA12714_Janus_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/PIA12714_Janus_crop.jpg/200px-PIA12714_Janus_crop.jpg 2x" width="100"/>

      </center>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
        History
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: History">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2280" data-file-width="1520" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg/220px-Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg/330px-Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg/440px-Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Titan4B_on_Launch_Complex_40.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Cassini-Huygens
         </i>
         on the launch pad
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini–Huygens
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s origins date to 1982, when the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Science_Foundation" title="European Science Foundation">
        European Science Foundation
       </a>
       and the American
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_National_Academy_of_Sciences" title="United States National Academy of Sciences">
        National Academy of Sciences
       </a>
       formed a
       <a href="/wiki/Working_group" title="Working group">
        working group
       </a>
       to investigate future cooperative missions. Two European scientists suggested a paired Saturn Orbiter and Titan Probe as a possible joint mission. In 1983, NASA's
       <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Solar_System_Exploration_Committee&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Solar System Exploration Committee (page does not exist)">
        Solar System Exploration Committee
       </a>
       recommended the same Orbiter and Probe pair as a core NASA project. NASA and the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       (ESA) performed a joint study of the potential mission from 1984 to 1985. ESA continued with its own study in 1986, while the American astronaut
       <a href="/wiki/Sally_Ride" title="Sally Ride">
        Sally Ride
       </a>
       , in her influential 1987 report
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ride_Report" title="The Ride Report">
         NASA Leadership and America's Future in Space
        </a>
       </i>
       , also examined and approved of the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       mission.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       While Ride's report described the Saturn orbiter and probe as a NASA solo mission, in 1988 the Associate Administrator for Space Science and Applications of NASA, Len Fisk, returned to the idea of a joint NASA and ESA mission. He wrote to his counterpart at ESA, Roger Bonnet, strongly suggesting that ESA choose the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       mission from the three candidates at hand and promising that NASA would commit to the mission as soon as ESA did.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       At the time, NASA was becoming more sensitive to the strain that had developed between the American and European space programs as a result of European perceptions that NASA had not treated it like an equal during previous collaborations. NASA officials and advisers involved in promoting and planning
       <i>
        Cassini–Huygens
       </i>
       attempted to correct this trend by stressing their desire to evenly share any scientific and technology benefits resulting from the mission. In part, this newfound spirit of cooperation with Europe was driven by a sense of competition with the
       <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">
        Soviet Union
       </a>
       , which had begun to cooperate more closely with Europe as ESA drew further away from NASA. Late in 1988, ESA chose Cassini–Huygens as its next major mission and the following year the program received major funding in the US.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
        <a href="#cite_note-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The collaboration not only improved relations between the two space programs but also helped
       <i>
        Cassini–Huygens
       </i>
       survive congressional budget cuts in the United States.
       <i>
        Cassini–Huygens
       </i>
       came under fire politically in both 1992 and 1994, but NASA successfully persuaded the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Congress_of_the_United_States" title="Congress of the United States">
        U.S. Congress
       </a>
       that it would be unwise to halt the project after ESA had already poured funds into development because frustration on broken space exploration promises might spill over into other areas of foreign relations. The project proceeded politically smoothly after 1994, although citizens' groups concerned about its potential environmental impact attempted to derail it through protests and lawsuits until and past its 1997 launch.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
        <a href="#cite_note-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
        <a href="#cite_note-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
        <a href="#cite_note-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
        <a href="#cite_note-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
        <a href="#cite_note-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Spacecraft_design">
        Spacecraft design
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Spacecraft design">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tr>
        <td class="mbox-image">
         <div style="width:52px">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
          </a>
         </div>
        </td>
        <td class="mbox-text">
         <div class="mbox-text-span">
          This section
          <b>
           needs additional citations for
           <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
            verification
           </a>
          </b>
          .
          <span class="hide-when-compact">
           Please help
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit">
            improve this article
           </a>
           by
           <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">
            adding citations to reliable sources
           </a>
           . Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.
          </span>
          <small>
           <i>
            (October 2014)
           </i>
          </small>
          <small class="hide-when-compact">
           <i>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">
             Learn how and when to remove this template message
            </a>
            )
           </i>
          </small>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2280" data-file-width="1521" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg/220px-Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg/330px-Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg/440px-Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cassini-Huygens_is_installed_to_the_payload_adapter.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <i>
          Cassini-Huygens
         </i>
         assembly
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The spacecraft was planned to be the second three-axis stabilized,
       <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">
        RTG
       </a>
       -powered
       <a href="/wiki/Mariner_Mark_II" title="Mariner Mark II">
        Mariner Mark II
       </a>
       , a class of spacecraft developed for missions beyond the orbit of
       <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">
        Mars
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was developed simultaneously with the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Comet_Rendezvous_Asteroid_Flyby" title="Comet Rendezvous Asteroid Flyby">
         Comet Rendezvous Asteroid Flyby
        </a>
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        CRAF
       </i>
       ) spacecraft, but budget cuts and project rescopings forced NASA to terminate CRAF development to save
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       . As a result,
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       became more specialized. The Mariner Mark II series was cancelled.
      </p>
      <p>
       Including the orbiter and probe, it has been the second-largest unmanned
       <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_spaceflight" title="Interplanetary spaceflight">
        interplanetary
       </a>
       spacecraft built,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa_quote_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-esa_quote-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as being among the most complex.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-esa_quote_31-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-esa_quote-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The orbiter had a mass of 2,150 kg (4,740 lb), the probe 350 kg (770 lb). With the launch vehicle adapter and 3,132 kg (6,905 lb) of propellants at launch, the spacecraft had a mass of 5,600 kg (12,300 lb). Only the two
       <a href="/wiki/Phobos_program" title="Phobos program">
        Phobos 1 and 2
       </a>
       spacecraft sent to Mars by the Soviet Union were larger.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       spacecraft was 6.8 meters (22 ft) high and 4 meters (13 ft) wide. Spacecraft complexity was increased by its
       <a href="/wiki/Trajectory" title="Trajectory">
        trajectory
       </a>
       (flight path) to Saturn, and by the ambitious science at its destination.
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       had 1,630 interconnected
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electronic_components" title="Electronic components">
        electronic components
       </a>
       , 22,000 wire connections, and 14 kilometers (8.7 mi) of cabling.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This is a very precise figure, which would benefit from a citation to back it up. (July 2015)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       The core control computer CPU was a redundant
       <a href="/wiki/MIL-STD-1750A" title="MIL-STD-1750A">
        MIL-STD-1750A
       </a>
       system. The main propulsion system consisted of one prime and one backup
       <a href="/wiki/R-4D" title="R-4D">
        R-4D
       </a>
       bipropellant rocket engine. The thrust of each engine was 490
       <a href="/wiki/Newton_(unit)" title="Newton (unit)">
        newtons
       </a>
       and the total spacecraft
       <a href="/wiki/Delta-v" title="Delta-v">
        delta-v
       </a>
       was about 2,040 meters per second.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Propulsion_33-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Propulsion-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Smaller monopropellant rockets provided attitude control.
      </p>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was powered by 32.7 kg
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krivobok_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Krivobok-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of
       <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">
        plutonium-238
       </a>
       —the heat from the material's radioactive decay was turned into electricity.
       <i>
        Huygens
       </i>
       was supported by
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       during cruise, but used chemical batteries when independent.
      </p>
      <p>
       The probe contained a DVD with more than 616,400 signatures from citizens in 81 countries, collected in a public campaign.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Until September 2017 the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       probe continued orbiting Saturn at a distance of between 8.2 and 10.2
       <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">
        astronomical units
       </a>
       from the Earth. It took 68 to 84 minutes for radio signals to
       <a href="/wiki/Time_of_arrival" title="Time of arrival">
        travel
       </a>
       from Earth to the spacecraft, and vice versa. Thus ground controllers could not give "real-time" instructions for daily operations or for unexpected events. Even if response were immediate, more than two hours would have passed between the occurrence of a problem and the reception of the engineers' response by the satellite.
      </p>
      <h2>

        </a>

        </span>

      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Summary">
        Summary
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Summary">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Instruments:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cassini_Orbiter_Instruments_38-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Cassini_Orbiter_Instruments-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <ul>
       <li>
        Optical Remote Sensing ("Located on the remote sensing pallet")
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cassini_Orbiter_Instruments_38-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Cassini_Orbiter_Instruments-38">
          [38]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <ul>
         <li>
          Composite Infrared Spectrometer (CIRS)
         </li>
         <li>
          Imaging Science Subsystem (ISS)
         </li>
         <li>
          Ultraviolet Imaging Spectrograph (UVIS)
         </li>
         <li>
          Visible and Infrared Mapping Spectrometer (VIMS)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        Fields, Particles and Waves
        <ul>
         <li>
          Cassini Plasma Spectrometer (CAPS)
         </li>
         <li>
          Cosmic Dust Analyzer (CDA)
         </li>
         <li>
          Ion and Neutral Mass Spectrometer (INMS)
         </li>
         <li>
          Magnetometer (MAG)
         </li>
         <li>
          Magnetospheric Imaging Instrument (MIMI)
         </li>
         <li>
          Radio and Plasma Wave Science (RPWS)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
       <li>
        Microwave Remote Sensing
        <ul>
         <li>
          Radar
         </li>
         <li>
          Radio Science (RSS)
         </li>
        </ul>
       </li>
      </ul>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Description">
        Description
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Description">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        '
       </span>
       s instrumentation consisted of: a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synthetic_aperture_radar" title="Synthetic aperture radar">
        synthetic aperture radar
       </a>
       mapper, a
       <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">
        charge-coupled device
       </a>
       imaging system, a visible/
       <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
        infrared
       </a>
       mapping
       <a href="/wiki/Spectrometer" title="Spectrometer">
        spectrometer
       </a>
       , a composite infrared spectrometer, a
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">
        cosmic dust
       </a>
       analyzer, a radio and
       <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
        plasma
       </a>
       wave experiment, a plasma spectrometer, an
       <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
        ultraviolet
       </a>
       imaging spectrograph, a
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">
        magnetospheric
       </a>
       imaging instrument, a
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetometer" title="Magnetometer">
        magnetometer
       </a>
       and an
       <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">
        ion
       </a>
       /neutral
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometer" title="Mass spectrometer">
        mass spectrometer
       </a>
       .
       <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">
        Telemetry
       </a>
       from the communications
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antenna_(electronics)" title="Antenna (electronics)">
        antenna
       </a>
       and other special transmitters (an
       <a href="/wiki/S_band" title="S band">
        S-band
       </a>
       transmitter and a dual-frequency
       <a href="/wiki/Ka_band" title="Ka band">
        K
        <sub>
         a
        </sub>
        -band
       </a>
       system) was also used to make observations of the atmospheres of Titan and Saturn and to measure the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        gravity
       </a>
       fields of the planet and its satellites.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Cassini Plasma Spectrometer (CAPS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The CAPS is a direct sensing instrument that measures the energy and electrical charge of particles that the instrument encounters (the number of electrons and protons in the particle). CAPS measured the molecules originating from Saturn's ionosphere and also determine the configuration of Saturn's magnetic field. CAPS also investigated
        <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
         plasma
        </a>
        in these areas as well as the solar wind within Saturn's magnetosphere.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
         <a href="#cite_note-40">
          [40]
         </a>
        </sup>
        CAPS was turned off June 2011 due to an electrical
        <a href="/wiki/Short_circuit" title="Short circuit">
         short circuit
        </a>
        that occurred in the instrument. The instrument was powered on in March 2012; after 78 days a second short circuit forced the instrument to be again shut down.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
         <a href="#cite_note-41">
          [41]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Cosmic Dust Analyzer (CDA)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The CDA is a direct sensing instrument that measures the size, speed, and direction of tiny dust grains near Saturn. It can also measure the grains' chemical elements.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
         <a href="#cite_note-42">
          [42]
         </a>
        </sup>
        Some of these particles are orbiting Saturn, while others may come from other star systems. The CDA on the orbiter is designed to learn more about these particles, the materials in other celestial bodies and potentially about the origins of the universe.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Composite Infrared Spectrometer (CIRS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The CIRS is a remote sensing instrument that measures the
        <a href="/wiki/Infrared" title="Infrared">
         infrared radiation
        </a>
        coming from objects to learn about their temperatures, thermal properties, and compositions. Throughout the
        <i>
         Cassini–Huygens
        </i>
        mission, the CIRS measured infrared emissions from atmospheres, rings and surfaces in the vast Saturn system. It mapped the atmosphere of Saturn in three dimensions to determine temperature and pressure profiles with altitude, gas composition, and the distribution of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerosols" title="Aerosols">
         aerosols
        </a>
        and clouds. It also measured thermal characteristics and the composition of satellite surfaces and rings.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Ion and Neutral Mass Spectrometer (INMS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The INMS is a direct sensing instrument that analyzes charged particles (like protons and heavier ions) and neutral particles (like atoms) near Titan and Saturn to learn more about their atmospheres. INMS was intended also to measure the positive ion and neutral environments of Saturn's icy satellites and rings.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-3">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
         <a href="#cite_note-43">
          [43]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
         <a href="#cite_note-44">
          [44]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Imaging Science Subsystem (ISS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The ISS is a remote sensing instrument that captures most images in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">
         visible light
        </a>
        , and also some infrared images and
        <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">
         ultraviolet
        </a>
        images. The ISS has taken hundreds of thousands of images of Saturn, its rings, and its moons. The ISS has a wide-angle camera (WAC) that takes pictures of large areas, and a narrow-angle camera (NAC) that takes pictures of small areas in fine detail. Each of these cameras uses a sensitive
        <a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">
         charge-coupled device
        </a>
        (CCD) as its
        <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetism" title="Electromagnetism">
         electromagnetic wave
        </a>
        detector. Each CCD has a 1,024 square array of pixels, 12
        <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">
         μm
        </a>
        on a side. Both cameras allow for many data collection modes, including on-chip data compression. Both cameras are fitted with spectral filters that rotate on a wheel—to view different bands within the electromagnetic spectrum ranging from 0.2 to 1.1 μm.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-4">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
         <a href="#cite_note-45">
          [45]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        <a href="/wiki/Spacecraft_magnetometer#Dual_technique" title="Spacecraft magnetometer">
         Dual Technique Magnetometer
        </a>
        (MAG)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The MAG is a direct sensing instrument that measures the strength and direction of the
        <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere_of_Saturn" title="Magnetosphere of Saturn">
         magnetic field around Saturn
        </a>
        . The magnetic fields are generated partly by the molten core at Saturn's center. Measuring the magnetic field is one of the ways to probe the core. MAG aimed to develop a three-dimensional model of Saturn's magnetosphere, and determine the magnetic state of Titan and its atmosphere, and the icy satellites and their role in the magnetosphere of Saturn.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-5">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
         <a href="#cite_note-46">
          [46]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Magnetospheric Imaging Instrument (MIMI)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The MIMI is both a direct and remote sensing instrument that produces images and other data about the particles trapped in Saturn's huge magnetic field, or magnetosphere. This information was used to study the overall configuration and dynamics of the magnetosphere and its interactions with the solar wind, Saturn's atmosphere, Titan, rings, and icy satellites.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-6">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
         <a href="#cite_note-47">
          [47]
         </a>
        </sup>
        MIMI includes the Ion and Neutral Camera (INCA), which captures and measures
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energetic_Neutral_Atoms" title="Energetic Neutral Atoms">
         Energetic Neutral Atoms
        </a>
        (ENAs).
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
         <a href="#cite_note-48">
          [48]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Radar
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The on-board radar is an active and passive sensing instrument that has produced maps of Titan's surface. The active radar can send radar waves able to penetrate the thick veil of haze surrounding Titan. By measuring the send and return time of the signals it is possible to determine the height of large surface features, such as mountains and canyons. The passive radar listens for radio waves that Saturn or its moons may emit.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-7">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Radio and Plasma Wave Science instrument (RPWS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The RPWS is a direct and remote sensing instrument that receives and measures radio signals coming from Saturn, including the radio waves given off by the interaction of the solar wind with Saturn and Titan. RPWS measures the electric and magnetic wave fields in the interplanetary medium and planetary magnetospheres. It also determines the electron density and temperature near Titan and in some regions of Saturn's magnetosphere. RPWS studies the configuration of Saturn's magnetic field and its relationship to Saturn Kilometric Radiation (SKR), as well as monitoring and mapping Saturn's ionosphere, plasma, and lightning from Saturn's (and possibly Titan's) atmosphere.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-8">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radio_Science_Subsystem" title="Radio Science Subsystem">
         Radio Science Subsystem
        </a>
        (RSS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The RSS is a remote-sensing instrument that uses radio antennas on Earth to observe the way radio signals from the spacecraft change as they are sent through objects, such as Titan's atmosphere or Saturn's rings, or even behind the
        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
         Sun
        </a>
        . The RSS also studies the compositions, pressures and temperatures of atmospheres and ionospheres, radial structure and particle size distribution within rings, body and system masses and the
        <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">
         gravitational field
        </a>
        . The instrument uses the spacecraft X-band communication link as well as S-band downlink and K
        <sub>
         a
        </sub>
        -band uplink and downlink.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-9">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4600" data-file-width="4600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg/220px-PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg/330px-PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg/440px-PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PIA18410-TitanSunsetStudies-CassiniSpacecraft-20140527.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         VIMS spectra taken while looking through
         <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Titan" title="Atmosphere of Titan">
          Titan's atmosphere
         </a>
         towards the
         <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
          Sun
         </a>
         helps understand the atmospheres of
         <a href="/wiki/Exoplanet" title="Exoplanet">
          exoplanets
         </a>
         (artist's concept; May 27, 2014).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Ultraviolet Imaging Spectrograph (UVIS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The UVIS is a remote-sensing instrument that captures images of the ultraviolet light reflected off an object, such as the clouds of Saturn and/or its rings, to learn more about their structure and composition. Designed to measure ultraviolet light over wavelengths from 55.8 to 190 nm, this instrument is also a tool to help determine the composition, distribution, aerosol particle content and temperatures of their atmospheres. Unlike other types of spectrometer, this sensitive instrument can take both spectral and spatial readings. It is particularly adept at determining the composition of gases. Spatial observations take a wide-by-narrow view, only one
        <a href="/wiki/Pixel" title="Pixel">
         pixel
        </a>
        tall and 64 pixels across. The spectral dimension is 1,024 pixels per spatial pixel. Also, it can take many images that create movies of the ways in which this material is moved around by other forces.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-10">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <dl>
       <dt>
        Visible and Infrared Mapping Spectrometer (VIMS)
       </dt>
       <dd>
        The VIMS is a remote sensing instrument that captures images using visible and infrared light to learn more about the composition of moon surfaces, the rings, and the atmospheres of Saturn and Titan. It is made up of two cameras in one: one used to measure visible light, the other infrared. VIMS measures reflected and emitted radiation from atmospheres, rings and surfaces over wavelengths from 350 to 5100 nm, to help determine their compositions, temperatures and structures. It also observes the sunlight and starlight that passes through the rings to learn more about their structure. Scientists used VIMS for long-term studies of cloud movement and morphology in the Saturn system, to determine Saturn's weather patterns.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-instruments-cassini_39-11">
         <a href="#cite_note-instruments-cassini-39">
          [39]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Plutonium_power_source">
        Plutonium power source
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Plutonium power source">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2271" data-file-width="1528" height="327" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg/220px-Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg/330px-Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg/440px-Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cassini%27s_RTG.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <i>
          Cassini
         </i>
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generators" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generators">
          RTG
         </a>
         before installation
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Because of Saturn's distance from the Sun,
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_cell" title="Solar cell">
        solar arrays
       </a>
       were not feasible as power sources for this space probe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solar-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       To generate enough power, such arrays would have been too large and too heavy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solar-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Instead, the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       orbiter was powered by three
       <a href="/wiki/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator" title="Radioisotope thermoelectric generator">
        radioisotope thermoelectric generators
       </a>
       (RTGs), which use heat from the natural decay of about 33 kg (73 lb) of
       <a href="/wiki/Plutonium-238" title="Plutonium-238">
        plutonium-238
       </a>
       (in the form of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plutonium_dioxide" title="Plutonium dioxide">
        plutonium dioxide
       </a>
       ) to generate direct current electricity via
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermoelectrics" title="Thermoelectrics">
        thermoelectrics
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_49-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solar-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The RTGs on the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       mission have the same design as those used on the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/New_Horizons" title="New Horizons">
         New Horizons
        </a>
       </i>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_(spacecraft)" title="Galileo (spacecraft)">
        <i>
         Galileo
        </i>
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_(spacecraft)" title="Ulysses (spacecraft)">
        <i>
         Ulysses
        </i>
       </a>
       space probes, and they were designed to have very long operational lifetimes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_49-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solar-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       At the end of the nominal 11-year
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       mission, they were still able to produce 600 to 700 watts of electrical power.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Solar_49-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Solar-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (One of the spare RTGs for the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       mission was used to power the
       <i>
        New Horizons
       </i>
       mission to
       <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">
        Pluto
       </a>
       and the
       <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">
        Kuiper belt
       </a>
       , which was designed and launched later.
       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        [
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2017)">
           citation needed
          </span>
         </a>
        </i>
        ]
       </sup>
       )
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="696" data-file-width="884" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg/220px-Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg/330px-Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg/440px-Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radioisotope_thermoelectric_generator_plutonium_pellet.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A glowing-hot plutonium pellet that is the power source of the probe's radioisotope thermoelectric generator
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       To gain
       <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">
        momentum
       </a>
       while already in flight, the trajectory of the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       mission included several
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_assist" title="Gravity assist">
        gravitational slingshot
       </a>
       maneuvers: two fly-by passes of
       <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">
        Venus
       </a>
       , one more of the Earth, and then one of the planet
       <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">
        Jupiter
       </a>
       . The terrestrial flyby was the final instance when the probe posed any conceivable danger to human beings. The maneuver was successful, with
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       passing by 1,171 km (728 mi) above the Earth on August 18, 1999.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA_JPL_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NASA_JPL-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Had there been any malfunction causing the probe to collide with the Earth, NASA's complete environmental impact study estimated that, in the worst case (with an acute angle of entry in which
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       would gradually burn up), a significant fraction of the 33 kg
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Krivobok_34-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Krivobok-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       of plutonium-238 inside the RTGs would have been dispersed into the Earth's atmosphere so that up to five billion people (i.e. almost the entire terrestrial population) could have been exposed, causing up to an estimated 5,000 additional cancer deaths over the subsequent decades
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eis_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-eis-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       (0.0005 per cent, i.e. a fraction 0.000005, of a billion cancer deaths expected anyway from other causes; the product is incorrectly calculated elsewhere
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
        <a href="#cite_note-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as 500,000 deaths). However, the chance of this happening were estimated to be less than one in one million.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eis_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-eis-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Telemetry">
        Telemetry
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Telemetry">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       spacecraft was capable of transmitting in several different telemetry formats. The telemetry subsystem is perhaps the most important subsystem, because without it there could be no data return.
      </p>
      <p>
       The telemetry was developed from ground up, due to the spacecraft using a more modern set of computers than previous missions.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
        <a href="#cite_note-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Therefore,
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       was the first spacecraft to adopt
       <a href="/wiki/Network_packet" title="Network packet">
        mini-packets
       </a>
       to reduce the complexity of the Telemetry Dictionary, and the software development process led to the creation of a Telemetry Manager for the mission.
      </p>
      <p>
       There were around 1088 channels (in 67 mini-packets) assembled in the
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       Telemetry Dictionary. Out of these 67 lower complexity mini-packets, 6 mini-packets contained the subsystem covariance and Kalman gain elements (161 measurements), not used during normal mission operations. This left 947 measurements in 61 mini-packets.
      </p>
      <p>
       A total of seven telemetry maps corresponding to 7 AACS telemetry modes were constructed. These modes are: (1) Record; (2) Nominal Cruise; (3) Medium Slow Cruise; (4) Slow Cruise; (5) Orbital Ops; (6) Av; (7) ATE (Attitude Estimator) Calibration. These 7 maps cover all spacecraft telemetry modes.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Huygens_probe">
        <i>
         Huygens
        </i>
        probe
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Huygens probe">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Huygens_(spacecraft)" title="Huygens (spacecraft)">
        Huygens (spacecraft)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tmulti tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:248px;max-width:248px">
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huygens_surface_color.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="630" data-file-width="316" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Huygens_surface_color.jpg/120px-Huygens_surface_color.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Huygens_surface_color.jpg/180px-Huygens_surface_color.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Huygens_surface_color.jpg/240px-Huygens_surface_color.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          <i>
           Huygens
          </i>
          view of Titan's surface
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px">
         <div class="thumbimage">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg">
           <img alt="" data-file-height="504" data-file-width="252" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg/120px-Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg/180px-Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg/240px-Huygens_surface_color_sr.jpg 2x" width="120"/>
          </a>
         </div>
         <div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">
          Same image with different data processing
         </div>
        </div>
        <div style="clear:left">
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <i>
        Huygens
       </i>
       probe, supplied by the
       <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">
        European Space Agency
       </a>
       (ESA) and named after the 17th century Dutch astronomer who first discovered Titan,
       <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">
        Christiaan Huygens
       </a>
       , scrutinized the clouds, atmosphere, and surface of Saturn's moon Titan in its descent on January 15, 2005. It was designed to enter and brake in Titan's atmosphere and parachute a fully instrumented robotic laboratory down to the surface.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The probe system consisted of the probe itself which descended to Titan, and the probe support equipment (PSE) which remained attached to the orbiting spacecraft. The PSE includes electronics that track the probe, recover the data gathered during its descent, and process and deliver the data to the orbiter that transmits it to Earth. The core control computer CPU was a redundant
       <a href="/wiki/MIL-STD-1750A" title="MIL-STD-1750A">
        MIL-STD-1750A
       </a>
       control system.
      </p>
      <p>
       The data were transmitted by a radio link between
       <i>
        Huygens
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       provided by Probe Data Relay Subsystem (PDRS). As the probe's mission could not be telecommanded from Earth because of the great distance, it was automatically managed by the Command Data Management Subsystem (CDMS). The PDRS and CDMS were provided by the
       <a href="/wiki/Italian_Space_Agency" title="Italian Space Agency">
        Italian Space Agency
       </a>
       (ASI).
      </p>
      <p>
       After
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
       <span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">
        's
       </span>
       launch, it was discovered that data sent back from the probe to European Space Agency's mission control center was largely unreadable. It was determined that
       <i>
        Cassini
       </i>
